# Learning Php

> PHP remains one most popular server-side languages used to build dynamic websites. Although it is not especially difficult to use, nonprogrammers often find it intimidating. This introductory course was designed to change that by teaching you PHP through a series of clear, focused, and easy-to-follow lessons. After briefly explaining what PHP is, instructor Joe Casabona introduces the features of 

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4) | Beginner | 35K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

### Introduction

#### Should you learn PHP?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/should-you-learn-php?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/should-you-learn-php?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Joe] One of the most popular languages for creating dynamic websites is PHP, but with the popularization of JavaScript tools, like React and Angular, is it worth investing your time in learning PHP?
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/should-you-learn-php?u=76281980&t=14)** The answer unequivocally is yes.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/should-you-learn-php?u=76281980&t=17)** For many web applications, PHP is very much an integral part of what makes them work.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/should-you-learn-php?u=76281980&t=23)** Front end tools like React can not talk to a database or provide a backend infrastructure like PHP can.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/should-you-learn-php?u=76281980&t=30)** Further, popular content management systems like WordPress and Drupal and websites like Facebook, Wikipedia, and Yahoo, all use PHP.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/should-you-learn-php?u=76281980&t=40)** In fact, 80% of all websites use at least some PHP.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/should-you-learn-php?u=76281980&t=46)** Learning PHP will give you the skills you need to develop and maintain dynamic, interactive websites.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/should-you-learn-php?u=76281980&t=53)** Hi, I'm Joe Casabona, a front end developer and educator who's been using PHP since 2003 and WordPress since 2004.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/should-you-learn-php?u=76281980&t=63)** So join me in my LinkedIn Learning course, which will show you everything you need to know to start using PHP.

#### Developing locally
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] While you will run PHP applications on a web server most of the time, setting a server up right now is not necessary.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=8)** Instead, we can start with local development.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=11)** A local development environment is generally free, or at least more affordable and faster than working with a server.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=19)** In fact, a local development environment is basically a web server running on your computer.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=26)** You also won't run the risk of crashing other websites on that server, so it's safer as well.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=34)** There are a few ways you can develop locally.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=36)** Depending on if you're on a Mac or PC, you can use MAMP or WAMP, respectively.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=48)** Both of these applications will set up an Apache MySQL and PHP, or AMP, server right on your computer.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=57)** You'll be able to manage files and view your web applications in the browser.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=62)** Now, there's a lot to know about AMP servers, and while you can install one of them now and quickly be up and running, if you want to learn more, you can check out the Apache MySQL and PHP courses in the LinkedIn Learning library.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=76)** These will give you more depth of knowledge on exactly what's happening on your computer.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=81)** But again, for now you can just install MAMP, for example, and start writing PHP code for this course right away.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=90)** You can also install an IDE, or integrated development environment.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=96)** This is a program that will run PHP code directly in the program.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=100)** No other tools are necessary.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=103)** There are several IDEs available for PHP.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=107)** A popular one is PhpStorm, which has a free 30 day trial.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=113)** A free alternative is NetBeans.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=116)** I'll be using VS Code to write the PHP, which I will then run in my browser using my local development environment.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/developing-locally?u=76281980&t=127)** No matter what you choose, setting up a local environment is a fast, safe way to learn PHP.

### 1. The Basics

#### How PHP works
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Joe] PHP stands for PHP Hypertext Processor, it's a recursive acronym.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=7)** But basically it's a programming language that allows you to add dynamic and backend functionality to your website.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=15)** It's often used for tasks that a markup language like HTML can perform; processing forms, talking to a database, creating secure areas, reading from a text file and more.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=30)** PHP is a server-side language, meaning our server or remote computer does all of the work and then sends the end results to our browsers.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=41)** You can imagine the process like a translator.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=44)** The server translates the PHP into a language our browsers can understand.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=51)** In other words, PHP allows you to apply logic and perform interactive tasks based on a set of criteria.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=58)** HTML strictly structures the data on a page.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=63)** HTML, CSS and JavaScript are also client-side languages, meaning our computers, specifically a web browser, does all of the processing, and we can see all the code.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=76)** That's not the case for PHP.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=79)** The strong benefit of that is that you can write more secure code.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=83)** Since not everything is exposed in the browser, you don't have to worry about showing passwords or other sensitive data.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=89)** You can retrieve and sanitize data before sending it to the browser.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=95)** The server knows which files use PHP because they end in .PHP, much like HTML files and in .HTML.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=105)** The server takes the PHP files, processes them, and sends only the output to the browser.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=113)** Further, the server knows only to process the PHP code.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=117)** That code will be between opening PHP tags and closing PHP tags.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=124)** The rest will be treated as plain text or HTML.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=128)** Because of this, you will see HTML and PHP commingling in the same files a lot.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=135)** But remember, while HTML can go into PHP files, PHP cannot go into HTML files.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/how-php-works?u=76281980&t=144)** Now that you know a little bit of how PHP works, let's start using it.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** php (16), html (9), css (1)
> **CLI Commands:** php (16)
> **Definitions:** stands for (1), is a  (1), in other words (1)
> **Code Keywords:** this, (1), let (1)
> **Analogies:** imagine (1)
> **Speakers:** - [joe] (1)

#### Printing on the screen
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The most common task you'll perform in php is printing stuff on the screen.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=5)** After all, your main goal is to create dynamic websites.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=10)** The simplest way to print something out on the screen is to use a php echo statement.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=15)** So I'll write my php opening tag, which is the less than sign, a question mark and then the letters php and then on a new line, I'll write echo hello world.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=27)** Then I'll close my php tags.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=31)** Echo tells php print out whatever follows this word.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=37)** You can also see that there are single quotes around hello world.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=41)** There are nuanced differences between a single and double quotes that you'll learn about later.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=47)** Finally, this statement ends in a semicolon.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=53)** This will be the case most of the time and you'll learn what the exceptions are later, but know that if you see an error, you may have missed a semicolon.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=64)** There are a few other ways that you may see information printed in php.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=71)** There's the shorthand echo tag, which is less than question mark equals and then hello world, which is a shorter way to write quick statements, but this is often not recommended by developers as it can be turned off on the server and that will lead to unintended results and errors.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=95)** There's also the print statement, which functions pretty much exactly like the echo statement except we use the word print instead of echo.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=108)** Echo is a little faster and a little more flexible, so it's more common.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=113)** That's what this course will use unless otherwise noted.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=116)** So let's look at what this produces in our browser.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=120)** You can see we have the words hello world three times, but there are no line breaks.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=127)** That's because php prints exactly what we tell it to.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=131)** Later, you'll learn how to produce your own line breaks and other special characters.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=137)** php code and html markup can also reside together in a single file on your server.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=143)** For example, here is a plain html file.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=147)** We can write this same output in php by adding php tags within the html.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=155)** We just need to make sure our file ends in dot php.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=159)** So here, we will use a basic echo statement to print out hello world.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=166)** Now we'll save this file and if we go back to our browser, we see the words hello world here and if we look at the source, we don't see any php code, we just see the resulting html.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=182)** Wrapping php commands, also known as statements, in html is a very common convention and there's one more that we should talk about and that's comments.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=192)** Comments are a way for you to write nonfunctional code in php.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=198)** In other words, you can leave notes to yourself or to other developers about what the code does.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=204)** With two forward slashes, you can write a single line comment or with a forward slash and then an asterisk, you can start a multi-line comment.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=220)** To end that multi-line comment, you would write asterisk and then a backslash.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/printing-on-the-screen?u=76281980&t=225)** Output is a first and crucial step towards writing php programs and now you have the very basics down.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### Using variables
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Printing information onto the screen is great, but we don't always know exactly what information we want to print while we're writing our code.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=9)** For example, if we want to print the visitor's name, there's no possible way of knowing that information before they visit.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=16)** That's where variables come in.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=18)** Variables are a way to store information in order to reference it later.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=23)** Variables can be used to store numbers, names, or other important data.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=29)** In PHP variables must start with a dollar sign, followed by a letter or an underscore, then an alphanumeric character string.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=39)** Here's an example.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=41)** You see that we have our opening PHP tags, a dollar sign followed by the word age, and then an equal sign, and some value, in this case 34, and we have our semi-colon.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=53)** Age is a placeholder for the number that we have stored in it, which is 34.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=60)** PHP knows age is 34 because of the equal sign, which means is assigned to or gets.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=68)** I'll usually say gets when referencing variables.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=71)** We can then use age as if it's the number 34.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=75)** Printing it, or even doing calculations on it, like adding, subtracting, or multiplying.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=82)** If we decide later we want to change it we can do that too.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=86)** Variables are easier to keep track of.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=89)** You can change them on the fly, and most importantly, like the opening example, sometimes you don't know certain information at runtime.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=96)** There are also special variables called constants that are defined a little differently.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=102)** In this case, you would use a function or a keyword called define with a parenthesis around it.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=111)** In the parentheses you will have the name of the constant in quotes, and in this case, it's version, then a comma and the value of that constant, which in this case is 1.1.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=124)** We can then treat the constant as if it's a variable without a dollar sign.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=129)** A couple of things to note though, are that constants are case sensitive.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=135)** So if we were to write version in lowercase here, it would not work.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=140)** And the important part, the reason that constants are called constants is because they are immutable, or they can't be changed at runtime.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=149)** So if we try to reassign a new value to version using either the gets symbol or the defined function, again, neither of those will work.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=162)** Constants are great if you have some value that you know you absolutely don't want to change throughout runtime.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=171)** In both cases for variables and constants, there are a set of predefined values that we can't use.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=180)** These are known as reserved words or predefined variables.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=184)** So for example, we can't use the word this in a variable assignment.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=190)** Similarly, we can't create a constant called PHP underscore version, or some other constants that you see here in the PHP manual.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=203)** The last important aspect to know about variables right now is that computers are very specific about how different types of data are represented.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=212)** Words are represented differently than numbers are.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=215)** And further whole numbers are represented differently than decimal numbers.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=220)** In programming languages you need to tell the computer, if you're giving it a word or a number.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=226)** Luckily PHP handles most of this for us.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=229)** We don't need to declare the kind of data our variables are most of the time.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=235)** PHP will figure out how we want to use it, and then do it for us.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=240)** This makes PHP a weak type language.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=244)** That said there are a few different ways variables can be represented, as integers which are whole numbers like zero, one, or 45.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=253)** Floats, which are numbers with decimal points.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=256)** For example, 1.0 or 3.16.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=260)** Characters are single letters, numbers, or symbols.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=265)** It's worth noting a few things about characters.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=269)** The first is that numbers with and without quotes are handled differently.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=274)** You can do calculations with the integer one, but not the character one, which is represented by quotes around the number one.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=285)** It's also worth noting that capital letters are considered different from lowercase letters.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=290)** So a capital A and a lowercase a are considered different by PHP.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=297)** This is why you are able to use upper and lowercase letters in your passwords to make them more secure.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=305)** The last two variable types here are strings, which are a collection of characters, such as hello, or Racer five.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=314)** And booleans, booleans can have only one of two values, true or false.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=321)** Now there is one more type of variable, and that's called an array.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=326)** Arrays are collections or sets of any of the previously mentioned variable types.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=332)** And you'll learn a lot more about those.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=334)** While integers, floats, and booleans can be written without the use of quotes, strings and characters must be placed in quotes.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=343)** So for example, we have a string with the variable name.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=348)** So we have name gets Joe, and we have a variable called first underscore letter.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=354)** So we have first underscore letter gets J.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=359)** You'll notice I used both single quotes and double quotes in the example above, and there are nuanced differences between them, but for assignments like these, we can use the single and double quotes interchangeably.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=372)** You might also notice that with first underscore letter there's no space.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=377)** Spaces are not allowed in variable names and often multi-word variables will replace spaces with an underscore.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=387)** Variables are an extremely important part of working with PHP.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-variables?u=76281980&t=392)** You will use them for storing and retrieving data, and it will make writing complex programs much easier.

#### Single vs. double quotes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So you can use single or double quotes for strings but what's the difference?
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=6)** Let's take a look.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=7)** The main difference between single and double quotes is any string in double quotes will be processed by PHP before it's outputted.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=16)** That means, for example, any variables in strings with double quotes will become their literal value.

>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=164)** So this actually brings up another issue because the PHP processor is going to print exactly what we write.

> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=174)** How do we print single or double quotes in strings?

> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=199)** The answer to that is escape characters.

>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=213)** If I refresh, we have no error and we have the statement.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=218)** In short, escape characters allow you to print out otherwise unprintable characters, like quotes, new lines and more.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=226)** They are primarily used with double-quoted strings, since that's where they are most necessary.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=233)** Double-quoted strings also recognize all escape characters, unlike single-quoted strings, which only recognize a couple.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=242)** You can find a full list of the escape characters on [php.net](https://php.net) but some of the important ones, the ones that you'll probably see the most often are on the screen here.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/single-vs-double-quotes?u=76281980&t=254)** \n for new line.
>

>

#### What are arrays and how do they work?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Arrays are a bit different from the rest of our variables.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=4)** Arrays act as key-value pairs that essentially function as a set of variables, so this allows us to make a list of related items that we can reference as one big variable.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=16)** The benefit of organizing data this way is it's easier to manage when we're working with related values or complex data sets, like when retrieving information from a database.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=28)** Array values can be any of the data types that standard variables can be, and we can mix them.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=34)** So we can have an array that includes strings only, an array that includes integers only, or an array that includes integers, strings, characters, or anything else you can think of.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=46)** There are two types of arrays you should know about, indexable arrays, which use numbered keys, and associative arrays, which use strings as keys.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=58)** You'll learn how to define each of those in PHP later but for now, let's learn a little bit about how they work as a concept.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=65)** Indexable arrays are basically numbered lists.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=68)** Each item in the array, or list, has an index assigned to it and that's how you reference it.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=75)** Items in an array are known as elements, but there's a small catch.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=81)** The indexing starts at zero, not one.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=85)** So if we have an array called colors and we want the first element in the array, we'd ask for the zeroth element.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=94)** That is, we'd use the index zero.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=97)** Let's look at a colors array with three elements.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=101)** This is an array of size three.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=104)** The indexes here are numbered from zero through two, and here's how you can visualize it.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=112)** We could say zero is red, one is blue, and two is green.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=119)** Understanding this concept is important because when you reference data in your arrays, you might get the wrong data.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=127)** If you're trying to get the third element, for example, and you ask for the index three item, you'll actually get an error saying the index is out of range or the offset is out of range.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=143)** The other type of array is an associative array.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=146)** This is an actual key-value pair.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=148)** It allows you to turn your numbered index into a string, so you can define the index as a string and then reference the element that way.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=156)** This makes keeping track of complex data a lot easier, and it works especially well if you want to store more than just a list of items.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=167)** If you want to associate two pieces of data, you can do that with an array.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=172)** Here's an example of names of people and their hometowns.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=177)** You can see that the numbered indexes are replaced with names.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=180)** So Joe is from Middletown, New York, Erin is from West Chester, Pennsylvania, Dave is from Exton, PA, and Brian is from Grand Rapids, Michigan.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=190)** So now we can essentially ask the array, what is Erin's hometown?
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=196)** Now, one more note before we get out of the abstract and start coding these arrays.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=201)** As mentioned earlier, array elements can take on any supported data type in PHP.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=208)** That includes arrays.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=211)** An array of arrays is called a multidimensional array, and these are especially common when working with databases.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=221)** You can have an entire table of data returned as an array where each row in the table is also in array, but we're getting ahead of ourselves.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-arrays-and-how-do-they-work?u=76281980&t=231)** Let's actually look at how to define arrays in PHP.

> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-display-basic-information-on-an-html-page?u=76281980&t=111)** So, I could have used single quotes here and then I wouldn't have had to use the escape characters.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-display-basic-information-on-an-html-page?u=76281980&t=118)** But this is one of Many solutions to the challenge.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-display-basic-information-on-an-html-page?u=76281980&t=122)** If we go look at this in the browser, you could see that, well, I am missing some text here, I forgot to add hello to the heading.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-display-basic-information-on-an-html-page?u=76281980&t=132)** So, save that, there we go.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-display-basic-information-on-an-html-page?u=76281980&t=134)** And this is printed out exactly as you would expect.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-display-basic-information-on-an-html-page?u=76281980&t=139)** If we look at the source, we'll see spaces between the paragraphs.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-display-basic-information-on-an-html-page?u=76281980&t=144)** So, I included the line break escape characters here, to make our source look a little bit more readable.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-display-basic-information-on-an-html-page?u=76281980&t=154)** If we hadn't included those line breaks, the source would be pretty much all on one line, it would still look like this in the browser, but the source would not be as readable.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-display-basic-information-on-an-html-page?u=76281980&t=166)** So, that's something to keep in mind as you write more and more complex websites, is that you'll want to format your source to be readable through the browser as well.

### 2. Control Structures

#### Boolean operators and the truth (update for PHP 8.0)
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/boolean-operators-and-the-truth-update-for-php-8-0-11628174?u=76281980)

#### Logical operators
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that you know how to compare numbers, there's another type of Boolean operator you should know about.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=5)** Logical operators.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=7)** Logical operators let us combine any number of Boolean statements to check the overall truthfulness of that statement.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=16)** No matter how many statements you combine, you'll still result in one Boolean value, true or false.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=23)** The first of these operators is the AND operator represented by two ampersands.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=29)** And this proceeds as true as long as all parts of the statement are true.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=36)** So for example, true AND true would evaluate to true.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=41)** It would be like saying, "The sky is blue "and the sun is yellow."
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=46)** However, as soon as any of those statements evaluates to false, the entire statement evaluates to false.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=54)** Again, that would be like saying, "The sky is blue and the grass is pink."
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=59)** Only half of that statement is true.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=61)** So the entire statement evaluates to false.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=66)** Then there's the OR operator which is represented by two pipe symbols.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=73)** An OR statement evaluates to true as long as any part of the statement evaluates to true.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=79)** So if both sides of the statement are true, the sun is hot or the snow is cold, That is true because at least some part of the statement is true.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=89)** However, if both parts of the statement are false, that is when an OR statement evaluates to false.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=96)** The sun is cold or snow is made of candy.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=99)** Neither of those statements are true.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=102)** So the entire statement is false.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=104)** It's also worth noting that AND and OR are commutative.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=108)** So true or false evaluates the same as false or true.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=113)** You are looking at the overall statement for truthfulness.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=117)** So let's look at a couple of examples in code.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=122)** Alright, my opening PHP statement.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=124)** And then I'll create a couple of variables.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=127)** We'll say A gets nine is less than five.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=132)** B gets 10 is not equal to 10.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=138)** C gets A and B.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=144)** D gets A or B.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=150)** E gets B and C.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=157)** And F gets A or D.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=165)** So now let's go ahead and evaluate all these.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=168)** And just to make it easier, let's put them in an array.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=173)** We'll call it logic.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=179)** And if you'd like to pause the video here and try to evaluate them yourself before you see the answers, go ahead.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=189)** So now we will print out this entire array using the print_r statement, and we'll wrap it in a pre block to make it just a little bit easier.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=205)** Save that and then refresh our page in the browser.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=209)** And you could see actually, this is an interesting quirk in PHP that when representing Boolean values, unless we explicitly tell PHP to, it's going to represent it as an integer where true is one and false is zero or null.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=228)** So what we actually want to do here is use a different function called var_dump which has a little bit more information than print_r.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=241)** And here it's a lot clearer.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=243)** That we see an array and A is true.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=248)** B is false.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=249)** C is false because B is false.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=253)** So that makes the entire AND statement false.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=255)** D is true because A is true.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=259)** E is false because C ends up evaluating the false.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=263)** And F is true because A is still true.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=268)** Now, it's worth noting that you can also use the words and and or for these operations.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=275)** So you could say something like C gets A and B.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=283)** But something interesting happens here.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=285)** We'll do var_dump on C so that we could see the entire statement.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=291)** And then we'll refresh.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=294)** So C is evaluating to true here even though C evaluates to false when we use the double ampersand.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=303)** That's because the double ampersand takes precedence over the assignment operator, but the word and does not.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=314)** So what's happening here is C is getting assigned to A, and this statement, C gets A and B is being evaluated.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=326)** So the entire statement evaluates to true.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=331)** To err on the side of caution in this course, we'll always use the double ampersand and the double pipe symbol.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=337)** Finally, there's one more operator you should know about.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=340)** And that's the caret symbol which is XOR.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=343)** XOR will evaluate to true as long as one statement but not all statements are true.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=352)** So for example, true XOR false is true.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=358)** False XOR true is true.
>
> **[6:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=361)** True XOR true XOR false is true.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=367)** But true XOR true is false and true XOR true XOR true is false.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=375)** Again, if all statements are true, then XOR will evaluate to false.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=381)** So again, logical operators will always evaluate to true or false.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=386)** And now that you understand Boolean operators, it's time to start putting them to use.

#### Creating if/else statements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Boolean statements can be used to help us decide what actions we want to take in our program.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=6)** We can show a user a message based on whether they're logged in or not.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=10)** We can perform a specific arithmetic operator based on a variable's value, and more.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=16)** To make those decisions, we need control structures.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=20)** Code that executes statements based on Boolean values.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=24)** The most common of these control structures is If statements.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=28)** An If statement will check for a condition to be true.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=31)** And if it's true, it will execute a specific set of code.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=35)** Here's a simple example.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=37)** If A is greater than B, echo A is greater than B.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=42)** This is a complete If statement.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=44)** We start with the keyword, If, we have parentheses and inside those parentheses we have a Boolean statement, in this case, a comparison operator.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=55)** Then we have an opening curly brace, the code we want to execute if that operator is true, and then the closing curly brace.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=65)** It says if this condition is true, execute the following code.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=69)** However, we can add more to that.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=71)** We could do something else if the condition evaluates to false.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=76)** So, here's a slightly extended If statement.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=79)** We have if A is greater than B, echo A is greater than B, else echo A is not greater than B.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=87)** So, after our basic If statement, we have the keyword else and then another set of curly braces.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=95)** Inside those curly braces we have the code that we want executed if the Boolean statement evaluates to false.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=104)** In between the if and the else, we can check for any number of other conditions.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=109)** So, you see here we have an even more complex If statement.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=114)** If A is greater than B, echo A is greater than B, else if B is greater than A, echo B is greater than A, else echo A and B appear to be equal.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=128)** So in between our if statement and our else statement, we have what is an else if statement.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=133)** We have the words else if, another Boolean statement, and then another set of curly braces in between which the code should execute if that second Boolean statement evaluates to true.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=148)** When you structure If statements like this, there are a few things to keep in mind.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=153)** A single "if" will always go first.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=156)** The first condition to evaluate as true will be the only one that runs and the rest will be ignored.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=164)** And the else statement always goes last.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=168)** It serves as a catch all if none of the conditions are true.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=173)** So, before we write our own If statement, let's take a look at a complex If statement we have here.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=179)** If 10 is less than one, we'll run some code, else if 10 is less than four, else if 10 is less than 11, else if 10 is less than 20, else.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=192)** So just by glancing at this, you know that 10 is not less than one, and 10 is not less than four, but 10 is less than 11 and 20.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=204)** So, we have at least two statements that will evaluate to true here.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=208)** If we run this code in our browser, let's see what happens.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=212)** It says we stopped at the second else if.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=216)** So we stopped at the second else if is here on line seven.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=219)** So if we go up to line six, we'll see this was the first condition that evaluated to true.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=226)** Even though 10 is also less than 20, our If statement kicks out and would pick up right here on line 14.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=236)** So if I write echo out of the if.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=240)** So if we refresh our page, we have we stopped at the second else if, and then out of the if, which is on line 14.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=249)** But If statements don't just need to use numbers either.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=253)** Let's say we want to create a situation where we show the user a message based on some status.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=260)** Let's create a variable called is logged in.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=264)** And we'll set that to true first.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=266)** So let's go ahead and write an If statement.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=269)** We'll say if is logged in.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=273)** This is all we need to include because is logged in is a Boolean.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=277)** So we'll have a true statement here.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=280)** Echo h3, Welcome back user, end h3, else echo, Welcome to the site.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=296)** Alright, and if we save this and then refresh our page We'll have welcome back user.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=302)** And if we set is logged in to false, the message will change to welcome to the site.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=311)** You can write If statement in another format as well and this is called alternative syntax.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=316)** You can see here that we have "if" the parentheses and the condition to check, but then we have a colon instead of a curly brace.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=326)** And then when we have our elseif, it's worth pointing out here that elseif is one word.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=333)** There's no space.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=334)** A different condition followed by colon, else followed by a colon and then the words elseif.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=342)** This alternative syntax is great, if you are printing out a lot of HTML, where you have to exit and enter the PHP tags a lot.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=352)** So in this example, we have if home_page, we'll say we have a variable called home_page, colon and then we close our PHP tags.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=363)** Then we print a big block of HTML.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=367)** Then we open our PHP tags again, and have the endif.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=372)** The alternative syntax makes reading longer form If statements, especially if there's a lot of HTML easier to read, because you no longer have to trace curly braces.
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=383)** You can look for the actual word endif.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=386)** And one more note about conditions in general, when checking the equality of a variable, I recommend using what's called Yoda conditions.
>
> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=396)** You place the variable on the right side of the condition.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=400)** This will prevent things like accidental assignments and bugs in your code.
>
> **[6:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=405)** So, a good way to write this is if 10 equals I, then do something.
>
> **[6:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=412)** And the bad way to write it is if I equals 10.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=416)** These are called Yoda conditions because they read a bit backwards like Yoda from "Star Wars" would speak.
>
> **[7:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=422)** If 10 this is then do something.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=425)** And just to drive the point home here, let's say we'll just use our, is logged in, variable here.
>
> **[7:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=433)** So we'll say I get 10.
>
> **[7:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=437)** So if we're using Yoda conditions, and we accidentally forget the second equal sign, and I refresh, we have an error.
>
> **[7:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=450)** However, if we're not using Yoda conditions, and I just say if I get 11.
>
> **[7:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=459)** This is going to evaluate to true even though we intended for I to be 10.
>
> **[7:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=464)** Because every assignment technically evaluates to true because it takes successful assignment.
>
> **[7:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=472)** So again, if we were to be using Yoda conditions here, this would prevent a parser and a logic error.
>
> **[8:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=480)** Both of which you'll learn about later.
>
> **[8:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/creating-if-else-statements?u=76281980&t=482)** But this makes for safer, more error free code.

> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1), is called (1)

#### Switch statements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] An alternative way of decision making is switch statements.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=4)** Where if statements allow you to check to see if a condition is true, switch statements allow you to more clearly make decisions based on the value of a single variable.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=13)** Here's a look at the syntax.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=16)** Let's say we have a variable called total that has the value 10.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=20)** We start with the keyword switch, followed by parentheses, and then the variable we're looking to evaluate, in this case, total.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=29)** We have our curly braces and then we have a case.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=33)** The keyword case is basically saying, does our variable in question equal the following value?
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=43)** So we have switch total case one is saying is total one.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=50)** If it is, we have a colon and then the code we want to execute.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=54)** In this case, we're printing out that total is one.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=57)** And then we have a break statement.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=59)** More on that later.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=61)** But we can continue to have as many case statements as we want.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=66)** So in this case we have case one and case two, and then we have the keyword, default.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=73)** Default is our catchall here, so if neither case is true, then we print out, total is more than two.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=82)** A few notes on switch statements.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=84)** Unlike if's, they don't terminate upon finding a true statement, and because of that, break statements are absolutely crucial.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=93)** A break statement will exit out of the switch statement for us.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=98)** Default keyword serves as a catchall, and you can group cases together.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=104)** So let's look at a few examples here.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=107)** So let's write out a simple example using what we just saw.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=112)** Total gets 10.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=113)** Switch, total, then we have our curly braces.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=118)** Case one, echo, put it in paragraphs.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=124)** Total is one.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=127)** Then we'll add our break statement.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=131)** And then we'll copy this and paste it a few times for different values.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=137)** And we'll go up to three in this example.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=142)** So case one, case two, total is two, and case three total is three.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=151)** Then we'll add our default.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=154)** Echo, total is more than three.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=161)** Now we don't need a break statement for default because that's going to be the last statement before we exit out of the switch completely.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=169)** So we'll save that, we'll refresh.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=171)** So we have total is more than three.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=175)** Now, just for argument's sake here, let's go ahead and change this to one and then get rid of the break statement here, and we'll see if anything happens.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=186)** We'll save this, refresh.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=188)** So total is one and then it prints total is two, and then we have the break statement, so the rest don't print, but if we don't have any of these, because one of the cases evaluated to true, we're going to have all of the values or all of the code execute.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=207)** So again, these break statements are crucial.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=212)** But unlike if statements, we don't necessarily just need to check for the truthfulness of a statement, right?
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=218)** So we can actually check strings as well.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=222)** Let's look at another switch statement where we want to assign a variable based on some other variable.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=231)** So I'm a big fan of the, "Ninja Turtles," so I'll say turtle, Leo, and then each Ninja Turtle has their own color of bandana.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=242)** So we'll set this to nothing for now, and then we'll write our switch statement.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=248)** So switch turtle, and then we can do the same checks, but now we can check for strings.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=254)** Case, Leo.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=257)** Bandana gets blue.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=261)** Break.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=262)** And then we'll repeat that process for the rest of the turtles.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=266)** Case, Raph.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=268)** Bandana, red.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=271)** Break.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=273)** Case, Mike.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=275)** Bandana, orange.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=279)** Break.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=282)** And case, Don.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=286)** Bandana, purple.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=290)** Break.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=291)** And then we don't necessarily need a default here, assuming that turtle can be one of these four values, so we'll just save that.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=301)** And then if we want to echo bandana here, do it in the paragraph.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=311)** It's going to be blue.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=314)** Now, a couple more points about switch statements here is that you can also group cases together.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=321)** So let's say we want to simplify our first switch statement here to say total is less than five.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=334)** What we could do is just get rid of the echo and break statements for cases one through three, and then we'll add case four here.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=350)** And then we can say total is five or more.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=356)** So again, we'll save this, we'll refresh.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=359)** Total is less than five, or if we change total to 10, total is five or more.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=365)** So we've combined case statements here, which is nice because now we don't have to worry about creating in better examples or some complex grouping of conditional statements.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=380)** And the last thing to mention here is that switch statements also have alternative syntax.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=386)** So we can replace the curly braces with a colon and an end switch, and we have the alternative syntax, which will work just like the curly brace syntax.
>
> **[6:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=401)** So when deciding which to use versus if or switch statements, consider if you're going to be checking for one of several values for a single variable, and not just the state of your program, like if the user is logged in, or if the user is on a specific page.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/switch-statements?u=76281980&t=418)** If you find yourself writing if statements that's like, if total is one, do this, if total is two, do this, a switch statement is better suited for that.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** switch (17), let (6), case, (6), this, (4), break. (4)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1), find (1)
> **Analogies:** just like (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)
