### 3. Math in PHP

#### Arithmetic operators
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There's no way around it, if you write programs, you will need to do some math.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=5)** It could be as simple as incrementing and detrimenting or you can do some pretty advanced calculations.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=11)** Heck, some gaming engines are made partially with PHP.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=16)** So to start, you can perform the four traditional arithmetic operations.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=20)** Addition with the plus sign, subtraction with the minus sign, multiplication with the asterix and division with a forward slash.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=30)** You can perform these operations in variable assignments or directly output them using echo.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=36)** So we could say, echo one plus one or numb gets one plus one and then echo numb, and in both instances, the result is two.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=47)** PHP is smart enough to recognize that we are using literal numbers here and not strings.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=52)** So, it evaluates the statement before the results are outputted.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=57)** And here are some examples of other operations, we could say A gets two and then echo A plus two, which will output four.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=67)** We could say B gets three, and then we can say, echo B minus A which will output one.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=74)** We could say C gets A times B and then echo C, which will output six.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=80)** And then we could say echo C divided by A, which will output three.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=87)** You can also negate a value by pretending the minus sign.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=91)** So like, negative three, so we can say numb gets 20 and then echo minus sign numb and the output would be negative 20.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=102)** You can even do complex mathematical operations using parentheses or exponents.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=107)** The rules of PEMDAS will usually apply, and as a refresher, PEMDAS stands for parentheses, exponents, multiplication, and division, and then addition and subtraction.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=122)** So, let's go out to our code editor now and look at a couple of examples.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=126)** Though parentheses are technically optional, they are very important to use them in your code to make operations explicit.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=135)** Let's look at an example where parentheses matter, so we'll open our PHP tag, and then we'll say echo five times six, plus three minus one, so we'll save this and then we'll refresh our page and the answer is 32.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=155)** Now, if we were to say, echo this statement again, but put parentheses around six plus three, refresh, and they're bumping up against each other so let's add a line break here, and the answer is 44.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=179)** So again, the parentheses are going to matter, similarly, if we put parentheses around five times six, we should still get 32 there, right?
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=189)** Because the operation is being done in order.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=194)** And just for one more example, if we do three plus five times six,
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=210)** we'll get 32.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=212)** So, even in this example, the rules of PEMDAS are applying.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=217)** And if you want to use exponent, you would do that with two asterisks, so, if we say echo five, asterix, asterix two, that's going to give us five squared, which is going to be 25, I'll get rid of these operators up here, and that's 25.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=236)** Now, let's look at one more complex equation where we have an exponent included.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=241)** So, we'll do five asterix, asterix two, times, we'll put the parentheses around six plus three so hopefully that will get done first because that's parentheses, we'll save and we get to 224.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=258)** If we remove the parentheses, we should get a different answer, 152.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=267)** So, these are the basic mathematical operators in PHP, but there are a few others that you should be aware of.

#### Figuring out even/odd with modulus
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There's one more arithmetic operation that's a little different from the rest because it doesn't give the actual results, it gives the remainder of dividing two numbers and it's called modulus or modulo.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=14)** It's represented by a present sign and it gives you the remainder of dividing two numbers.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=20)** So if we have a gets two and b gets three and we echo b modulus a, that will print out one because when we divide three by two, we have a remainder of one.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=38)** Similarly, if we were to echo 15 modulus nine, the remainder would be six.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=44)** This is often used to find out if some number is odd or even.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=49)** So if we write some code here where a gets five if a modulo two equals zero, then echo a is even else echo a is odd.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=74)** Close my paragraph tag up here.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=78)** If you divide any even number by two, the remainder will be zero.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=82)** Now this was used for a long time programmatically to apply CSS classes to even table cells or even rows, but now there is a CSS selector to do that and that would be the recommended way is to use the CSS selector, but there still could be some utility in this if you're trying to figure out the evenness or oddness of a number.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=107)** You can also see if one number is the factor of another number.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=111)** So let's just reassign our variables here.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=114)** I'll say a is 15 and b is five.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=120)** So if we say echo and then we'll use a ternary operator here.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=127)** a modulo b equals zero add our dollar sign here so we don't get an error.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=137)** We could say b is a factor of a, otherwise b is not a factor of a.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=150)** Okay, so if we save this.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=153)** Check all of our syntax here, we missed a semi-colon here.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=156)** So everything's looking good.

>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=165)** Finally, you can get the negative remainder by negating the dividend.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=171)** That is the first number in the expression.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=174)** So if I say echo negative a modulo and I can't say b here because I know that b is a factor of a, so we'll get zero.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=187)** I'll just say two.
>

>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/figuring-out-even-odd-with-modulus?u=76281980&t=195)** So modulus will return the remainder after we do a division and it's often used to figure out if a number is odd or even or if the number is a factor of a another number.

#### Incrementing and decrementing variables
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Arithmetic operators allow you to enumerate as needed.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=4)** Perhaps you want to count the number rows in the table or create a numbered list.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=9)** To increment based on what you know at this point, you would use the first line of code here.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=15)** A get a plus one.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=17)** This takes the variable a, adds one to it and reassigns the new value back to a, and the same goes for decrementing.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=24)** You could say b gets b minus one.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=26)** But there's a slightly shorter way that you can do this using a ++ or -- symbol.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=33)** So the second line you see on the screen here, a++, would increment a by one.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=41)** Similarly, b-- would decrement b by one.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=47)** And you don't have to use these in just assignments either.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=50)** You can add them to echo statements as well.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=53)** Just bear in mind a few things.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=56)** If you use this shorthand anywhere, including echo statements, the variable will still change values.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=63)** So you can't simply say echo a++ and assume a retains its previous value.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=70)** As a matter of fact, depending on how you do it, you'll get different results.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=75)** And there are pre and post-increment actions.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=78)** So let's actually take a look at those.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=81)** Here we have a block of code and on line eight, I have exit just to make sure that these lines just print just yet.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=89)** So first, we have the post increment action.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=93)** A is five and then we say the value of a is a++.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=99)** This is going to still be five because PHP is processing a and then processing the ++.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=107)** And then a will be six.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=110)** So let's run this code.

>

>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=125)** Then it's incrementing it.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=127)** So the next time that we print a, which is on line six, a will be incremented.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=135)** Now let's clear these results and we'll just move our pre-increment actions up.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=147)** Okay, so now a is five.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=150)** And this time, we're putting the ++ on the left side of a, which means that PHP is processing the ++ first and then printing a.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=161)** So this will print six and then a will remain six even though we didn't do a formal assignment, we just used the increment operator.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=170)** So again, we'll print this out.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=172)** The value of a is six.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=173)** And it remains six.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=177)** The same thing goes for decrementing.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=178)** So we can decrement here and a will be four and then remain four.

> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=184)** And if we remove our exit statement to do the post decrement, the value of a is five 'cause it got reset on line nine.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=196)** And then it is four because the post decrement happened after the first print.

> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=217)** So if I have A and then I say echo a is, concatenate ++a, I've got to do the pre-increment here and then I'll add the line break statement.

>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=240)** And further, we can use the shorthand arithmetic operations for assignments for more than just adding and subtracting, as well as more than just a single increment.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=254)** So if I say a gets one, and then I reassign a by saying plus gets five, a will now be six right here.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=268)** If I say a- gets five, it will be -4.

> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=273)** If I say asterisk gets five, which is a multiply gets and then I refresh, a is five.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=283)** And then if I say a / gets five, which is a divided by assignment, a is .2.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=294)** So this shorthand is especially useful for incrementing and decrementing as you learn other control structures like loops.

> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/incrementing-and-decrementing-variables?u=76281980&t=303)** But the shorthand syntax is a great way to write succinct operations and assignments.

>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=116)** This is a pretty complicated one because there's a lot of stuff going on inside the parentheses.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=122)** So first, we need to say five times at our paren and then we have 12 divided by two minus eight times four plus 12 times six.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=135)** We want to make sure to get parentheses around all of the multiplication and division.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=140)** So we'll add our first parentheses 12 divided by two and then minus parentheses eight times four close parentheses plus 12 times six in another set of parentheses and that appears to be our entire statement.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=164)** So now let's go ahead and print out the results.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=168)** We'll want the results to be on a nice neat line here, so I will echo pre-print our results
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=180)** echo close pre and let's see how we did here.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=188)** All right, 6,400, negative 1,530 and 230.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=195)** So there you go.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=196)** Those are the results for this challenge.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=198)** You may have done things a different way with different parentheses.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-implementing-a-complicated-calculation?u=76281980&t=202)** One of the important things to take away from this challenge is that depending on the version of PHP you're using, you may run into some problems and so making the parentheses as explicit as possible will always help with your operations.

### 4. Loops

#### What are loops?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Sometimes you want to repeat a task until it's done.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=4)** Practically, this could be looking at all of the records in a file or continuously incrementing a number until you hit the limit you want.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=11)** The control structures you've seen so far focus on making a decision and then executing code once, but there is a different control structure for if you want to repeat a task some number of times and that's called a loop.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=26)** Loops will execute a piece of code until some condition becomes false.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=31)** So you can imagine the structure is like this.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=35)** While some condition is true, execute some lines of code.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=41)** If some condition becomes false, exit the loop and move on.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=47)** there are three different types of loops you'll learn about, but they all have two important components for you to keep in mind.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=55)** They all must have a true/false condition, like the ones that we saw in if statements and they all must include a statement that moves the condition towards being false.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=67)** For example, if you have a structure like this where i is one and the condition is i is less than 10, within the code your loop is executing, you should have the statement i plus plus because that increment i and eventually moves the statement i is less than 10 towards being false because i will eventually get to 10, which evaluates to false.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=97)** If you don't have a condition that moves towards false, you will end up with an infinite loop.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=105)** These are loops that never terminate and they will crash your website or your application, but luckily, there are a few loops with safeguards to make sure that doesn't happen.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-loops?u=76281980&t=116)** So let's take a look at three types of loops in PHP, while loops, for loops and foreach loops.

#### Do/while loops
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The most common form of loop is a do while loop.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=4)** These will continue to run until some condition becomes false.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=9)** They are general purpose loops that can be used for anything.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=12)** And there are actually two types of syntax for these loops.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=16)** The first uses the keyword "do."
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=19)** So we'll add our php tags.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=22)** Let's create some condition here.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=25)** We'll say i is zero, and then we'll start our loop.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=28)** So this could say, do echo, i, while i is less than 10.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=40)** So this loop will execute and print out the value of i, as long as i is less than 10.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=47)** So we should see the numbers zero through nine, printed out on the page.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=52)** But we don't.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=53)** And you could see that our program is still running here.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=57)** That's because we forgot one crucial part of the loop, which is the incrementor.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=63)** So if we add, i++ save and refresh.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=69)** Now, we have a list of numbers from zero through nine, and then our loop terminates.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=75)** Do while loops are great if you want some condition to run at least once.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=80)** For example, maybe you want to add a loop to a number guessing game, where you evaluate a guess and while the guess is wrong, you can allow the user to keep guessing.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=93)** In that instance, you would want to evaluate the guess at least once.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=99)** However, most of the time you will only want the code to execute if the condition is true.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=107)** In that case, we can essentially turn our do while loop upside down and say while i is less than 10, do some code.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=117)** So you can just copy this right here.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=122)** And then this will have the same results as our do while loop.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=128)** However, if we set i to 10, save this and then refresh,
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=136)** you'll see that only the number 10 prints out.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=142)** That's because even though the do while loop has a condition for i is less than 10, the code is going to execute at least once.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=149)** Whereas with the while loop, this condition is never true.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=154)** So those are the two versions of while loops.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=157)** While loops are great for lots of things like checking to see if some state is true or false, and then executing code based on that like with our number guessing game.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/do-while-loops?u=76281980&t=167)** In the instance we have here where we're simply incrementing and printing out the value of i, there's actually a better suited kind of loop for this, and that's called a for-loop.

#### For loops
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For loops are made specifically for enumeration.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=4)** The starting assignment, the condition, and the incrementer are all in the for loop definition.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=10)** So you see here, we have the keyword for, and then in parentheses, we have a starting assignment followed by a semi colon, a condition followed by a semi colon, and then the incrementer.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=21)** So inside the loop, all we need is the code we want to execute.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=26)** For comparison sake, let's take a look at our while loop again.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=30)** You can see we have the starting assignment outside the loop.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=34)** We have the condition in the parentheses and we have the incrementer inside the while loop.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=40)** The incrementer as you saw can be pretty easy to forget.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=45)** Now let's write a for loop that does a similar task.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=49)** We'll write for and then we have the assignment, I gets zero.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=53)** We have our condition, I is less than 10, and then we have our incrementer.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=60)** Now it's important to note here that even though the incrementer is included in the loop definition, it's not enforced by the PHP compiler.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=71)** So you can leave it off and still have a valid for loop.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=75)** It's also worth noting that we can include or exclude the semi-colon here, I'm going to exclude it.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=81)** Okay, so now we have our for loop let's copy the same echo statement we have in our while loop.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=87)** Now, what we will see here is a list of numbers from zero through nine, but we'll only see it once because we started I at 10 up here so this while loop will never execute.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=101)** We'll save this and refresh the page.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=104)** And we have a single list of numbers zero through nine.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=109)** Again, for loops are perfect for incrementing or for moving through a numbered list or doing some tasks like that because everything is managing the flow of the loop in a single line that serves for the loops definition.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=126)** It's also worth mentioning that I'm calling the third expression in this loop, the incrementer, but it could easily be a decrementer.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=134)** So we can have the reverse here.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=137)** I gets 10, I is greater than zero, I minus minus.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=143)** Now we should have a reverse version of this loop, starting from 10 and going to one.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=148)** And again, just because the condition or the incrementer is included, doesn't mean that we are prevented from having infinite loops.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=158)** For example, if I change this incrementer back to an actual incrementer, our loop will never become false here because I starts at 10 which is more than zero and never moves towards zero.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=174)** So now we have an infinite loop continually printing the numbers from 10 up to infinity until our program eventually crashes or stops executing.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=187)** So we'll change that back.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=189)** Now, let's take a look at one more example of a for loop where we are traversing through an array.

>

>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=237)** And we want I to be less than the size of the array.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=241)** So the array has for elements in it, which makes it an array of size for, but we don't always know the size of the array.
>

>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=258)** This will return for.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=261)** So this is saying I is less than the size of the colors array which is for.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=266)** And I plus plus.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=269)** Now inside the loop, we can print out each item of the array because we are keeping track of our index.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=275)** So we could say echo P I'll use concatenation here to make the syntax of the array a lot clearer than if we were using quotes and P.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=288)** And now we should get a list of each color in the array, red, blue, green, yellow.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=294)** There are a few things to keep in mind as you write a loop like this.

>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=302)** So if you start at one, you will eventually get an error and you'll skip the first element in the array.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=308)** The size of function sends the number of elements in the array and not the highest number index.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=315)** So in this case size of is four, but the highest number index is three.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=321)** If you don't do your math exactly right.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=323)** you will get an error in your code and your program will crash.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=327)** Now you will probably traverse through a lot of arrays.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=331)** That's how a lot of data is sent back, especially when you start working with files or databases.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=336)** And luckily when it comes to arrays, you don't need to do most of this because PHP has a loop specifically for arrays.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/for-loops?u=76281980&t=345)** The for each loop.

#### Working through arrays with foreach loops
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Foreach loops are a little different from the other loops you've seen.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=4)** They're designed specifically for traversing through arrays.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=9)** So we have two types of syntax here.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=12)** We have foreach array as item and then we can do something with the item or we have foreach array as key arrow value for arrays with key value pairs.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=25)** Let's take a look at some examples.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=28)** So on the screen we have the for loop and we're going to convert this to a foreach loop.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=34)** We have our colors array defined on line two.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=37)** So we could say foreach colors as color and then echo paragraph color.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=49)** So now we should get two lists of colors: red, blue, green, yellow, red, blue, green, yellow.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=56)** What this foreach loop is essentially saying is take each item in the colors array and assign it to the variable color one at a time, then print that color.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=67)** The loop will end when there are no more elements in the array.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=71)** There is much less room for error here than with a for loop.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=76)** We don't need to worry about indexes, array sizes, or anything like that.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=82)** PHP takes care of it all for us.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=86)** This also works with associative arrays.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=88)** So let's use the hometown arrays we saw earlier.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=91)** You can find this in the exercise files for the arrays lesson.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=98)** The way we would loop through this is by saying foreach home_towns as and then we would mean the key and the value.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=107)** So in this case, we could say name and then town.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=113)** Then we can have our echo statement.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=115)** So we'll say echo name lives in town.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=122)** We'll save that. And then refresh our page.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=125)** Joe lives in Middletown. Aaron lives in Westchester.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=128)** Dave lives in Exton. Brian lives in Grand Rapids.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=132)** Foreach loops make this a lot more manageable.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=134)** We don't need to figure out how to do this with a while loop or a for loop, especially when it comes to big complex arrays like the brothers array we saw earlier.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/working-through-arrays-with-foreach-loops?u=76281980&t=145)** This will come in handy, especially when you're working with files and databases.

> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/challenge-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=18)** So the first few entries in the Fibonacci sequence would be one, one, two, three, five, eight, and so on.

#### Solution: Calculating the Fibonacci sequence
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, so here's the solution to the challenge of printing every number in the Fibonacci sequence without going over 200.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=14)** And we put a limit on there because the Fibonacci sequence goes on infinitely, right?
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=18)** So there are a lot of solutions to this problem, this is a pretty common computer science problem and therefore, a lot of beginner programmers think about it.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=28)** But we're going to do this in a very straight forward way.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=31)** So we're going to use three variables here, current, which we'll start at one because that's where the Fibonacci sequence starts, previous, which is going to be zero because we're going to need to add some previous value to the first entry and next, which we'll set to null for now.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=55)** Then we'll have a while loop.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=57)** We'll say while current is less than 200 and we can always put the limit in a variable as well, so we could say limit gets 200 just in case we ever want to change this, or we need to reference it somewhere later.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=71)** So while current is less than limit, let's do some things.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=75)** First, we're going to echo the current value.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=82)** So we'll say echo current, and then we want to have a comma separated list of these values so that's what we'll add after current.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=93)** Then we need to do some math, so we'll say next gets current plus previous add a semi-colon, then we'll say previous gets current and then current gets next.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=112)** Now there might be a pithier way to do this without using the next variable, but to do it in the most straightforward manner, we're going to use these three because you could run into a few weird off by one bugs if you don't.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=130)** So there we go, there's our solution, let's see how well it does.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=135)** I'll save this and refresh in the browser.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=137)** One, one, two, three, five, eight, 13, 21, 34, 55, 89, 144 and if we were to add 144 and 89, that would definitely go over 200.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=149)** So we stopped in the right place.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=152)** Now, the last thing that we might want to do, right, is there is now an extra comma here.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=159)** So if we want to take it one step further, we could actually create a new variable called sequence and set that to nothing.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=171)** And then we can replace our echo statement with a variable assignment.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=175)** So we could say sequence. get you saw the arithmetic assignments in an earlier video, but we can also do that with strings.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=186)** So we can use the concatenation operator with the assignment operator to say append this to the end of the last string.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=196)** So with that, what we can do is a few fancy things on sequence once we exit the loop.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=204)** The first thing we can do is trim all white space so there is a built in function called trim in PHP that will remove white space from the beginning and end of a string.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=218)** So that will take care of the last space.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=220)** And then we have one character that we want to get rid of, and that is the ending comma.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=226)** So, again, we can use another built in function for that, we could say sequence gets substr so that we'll send a substring back to us and that sub string is going to be based on the string we send it.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=241)** An integer as a starting point, starting at zero and then the length of a string.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=247)** So we are going to send sequence.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=252)** The starting point is going to be zero strings are indexed, just like arrays and then the length.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=260)** There's another built in function in PHP called strlen, which functions a lot like size of for arrays that is going to accept a string.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=271)** So we'll send that sequence, but we want this to be the length of the string minus one.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=279)** So with that, we'll end this statement just to run through this again, this says sequence gets a substring of sequence starting at zero, and then going to the full length of the string minus one.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=296)** Now, if we echo sequence, we have our Fibonacci sequence with no ending comma.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-calculating-the-fibonacci-sequence?u=76281980&t=303)** Now we could eliminate line 19 and say minus two, that would give us the same results but now we have a fully functioning Fibonacci sequence, never going over 200 that is also syntactically correct.

### 5. Functions and Objects

#### What are functions?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Functions are reusable snippets of code that can be called multiple times.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=6)** You can imagine functions as variables for blocks of code.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=10)** And the reason that we would use functions is when we want to reuse a snippet of code more than once, instead of rewriting those multiple lines of code every time, and then having to change them if we ever want to update those lines of code, we can put them in an easy to use function.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=31)** Functions can do one of two things.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=33)** They can return a variable or a value that can be used as or assigned to a variable, or they can print something out.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=43)** Here's the function syntax for printing something out.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=47)** We have the keyword function and then the name of our function, the name of the function should be formatted similarly to the name of our variables, where spaces are replaced by underscores.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=61)** But notice there's no dollar sign in front of the function name.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=64)** Instead, the function name has an opening and closing parentheses after it.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=70)** Then we have our curly braces and in between the curly braces, we have the code that we want to execute as the function.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=78)** In this case, our function is printing out Hello World, and you could see it in use below.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=85)** So we have our paragraph tag and then our opening PHP tags.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=90)** We have the function name, Hello World with the parentheses and a semi-colon, our closing PHP tag, and then our closing paragraph tag.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=100)** This will print out a paragraph with the text Hello World.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=104)** A function can also return a value.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=105)** So again, we have our keyword function, the name of the function, Hello World, with the parentheses, the curly braces.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=113)** And the only difference is that instead of the word echo, we have the keyword return, which basically says treat whatever you are sending back from this function as a variable.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=128)** So in this case, we are using the word echo.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=131)** Then we have our paragraph tag in quotes, the concatenation operator, our function call, Hello World, a concatenation operator, and then our closing paragraph tag with the semi-colon.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=144)** So in this case, the function name, Hello World is being treated as a variable with the value Hello World.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=152)** If a function were to return a value, it could be treated as its own variable or assigned to a separate one.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=158)** So let's take a look at a simple example where a function returns a Boolean.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=163)** We have a function here called is bigger and inside it returns the Boolean operator 10 is greater than or equal to five, which will be true.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=175)** Then below that we assign our variable bigger to whatever is returned from is bigger.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=183)** So we're saying bigger gets is bigger, the function.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=188)** And then we can use that variable and an if statement, but we can also just call the function as the condition is the if statement and skip the variable assignment altogether.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=199)** So we have, if is bigger than echo, one thing, otherwise echo another thing.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=206)** So here the function is being treated as a variable.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=210)** Finally, you might be wondering why we have parentheses for the function name.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=215)** Well, we can pass variables or arguments to functions.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=221)** So our is bigger function is pretty useless if it's only evaluating 10 is greater than or equal to five, we might want something more dynamic than that.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=231)** So you could see the syntax here, function is bigger, and then inside those parentheses, we have two arguments, A and B.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=241)** A and B will take on the value of whatever we pass to the function.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=246)** So in our assignment, we have bigger gets is bigger and then 10 and five.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=254)** So in this instance, 10 will be assigned to A and five will be assigned to B.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=259)** And functions can do more than just return Boolean values.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=263)** Anything that can be stored in a variable can be returned from a function.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=267)** The last thing it's worth noting here is that functions need to be defined before they are called, just like variables.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=276)** So if we were to swap this syntax and have bigger gets is bigger before the function declaration, PHP would throw an error because it doesn't yet know what is bigger is now.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=288)** This is how we rate our own functions, but PHP has a bunch of built in functions as well.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=294)** You've seen a few of those already, like size of and strlen.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=299)** These will help you accomplish tasks very efficiently, like formatting the date with the date, grabbing the size of an array and more.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-functions?u=76281980&t=307)** Let's look at PHPs' built in functions now.

#### Using built-in functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - PHP has an extensive library of functions to help us complete certain actions more efficiently.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=6)** You saw it with the size of function earlier, without that function, we'd have to write our own much more resource, heavy way to get the number of elements in an array.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=17)** But how do you find these built in functions?
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=21)** A good starting place is the phpmanual@[php.net](https://php.net).
>

> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=39)** However, there are a few caveats, the search isn't great.

>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=181)** Let's not worry about the time for now.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=184)** One note about the documentation is that any arguments in square brackets are optional and you can see the default value with an assigned statement.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=195)** So if we don't send a timestamp PHP, we'll grab the current time and use that.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=201)** So let's say we wanted to print a date with the full name of the month, followed by a space and the two digit day followed by a comma and the four digit year let's look at this handy table on the format section.

> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=221)** D if we scroll down to month, the full name of the month is represented by the capital letter F and year is represented by the capital letter Y again, we want the four digit year.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=236)** So if we go back to our editor and we say eco date, we're going to write a simple string with the characters.

> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=246)** We just pointed out F space D comma, Y then if we go view this in the browser, we'll see the current date.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=256)** If at any time you want to use any of the format characters as the literal character in the date function, you could always use the backslash character to escape them.

#### Anonymous functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Another way to write functions is anonymously.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=4)** Anonymous functions don't get a name and are often created at runtime to perform a specific task.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=10)** This is slightly more efficient than defining a function if you're only going to use it once.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=16)** These are also called closures or callback functions.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=20)** The latter because sometimes functions in PHP require a different function as the second argument.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=28)** PHP is basically saying, we will do some of the work, but you need to tell us how to do it.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=34)** One common example of this is the usort function in PHP.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=39)** You could see that it accepts an array as its first value and it is being passed by reference and then, the second argument is a callable compare function.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=54)** So usort will sort the array, but you need to tell it how you want to compare the items.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=60)** Since we're only going to use this function for usort, it doesn't make sense for us to define a separate function and keep it in PHPs memory if we're only going to use it once so we are going to write an anonymous function.

>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=155)** Now PHP actually treats strings as a raise so we can reference each character by the index.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=163)** Since indexing starts at zero, the second character in the string will be one.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=168)** So we'll say return a one and then we can use a spaceship operator.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=174)** This is a relatively new operator in PHP that will do a greater than, less than and equal to comparison all in one shot and return a value.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=187)** So we're saying a one spaceship operator b one if in the event that the second character in a is greater than or equal to the second character in b, then the spaceship operator will return a, but if b is greater than a, the spaceship operator will return b.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=213)** So with this sort function, let's go ahead and print out our array and we will add the pre-tags first and I'm actually going to do this a different way this time.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=223)** I'm going to close our PHP and then I will open it again after I write the pre-tag, print r names and then I'll close the pre-tag.

>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=237)** I'll refresh the page.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=239)** So let's see, we've got E-O-O-R.

>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/anonymous-functions?u=76281980&t=256)** Anonymous functions are used in pretty specific cases, but they are great to know about as you'll likely come across them, especially if you need to do something like sort objects.

#### What are objects and classes?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Functions are a great way to have consistency throughout your code base.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=5)** And you can reuse useful code.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=7)** Another way to do that is with classes.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=10)** A class is a way of grouping variables and functions together as a single referenceable unit.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=16)** The powerful part of classes is you can have more than one as long as you name them differently.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=21)** The different instantiations or versions of these classes are called objects.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=27)** And the benefit is that you can have a single set of variables and functions and based on the object, reference them.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=36)** PHP has many built-in functions but we can also write our own.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=41)** So if you have a Person class, you can use it to create two Person objects, Alice and Bob.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=48)** They'll have the same variables and functions available to them but the information will be different.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=55)** You can think of classes as the mold for a toy.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=59)** Then each individual toy with different color plastic and paint are the objects.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=65)** Variables in classes are called properties.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=68)** So in our Person class, we might have properties for a first and last name, occupation, and age.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=74)** Functions in classes are called methods.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=77)** We can use methods to access properties, remember scope comes into play with classes as well, and to perform tasks specific to an object's information.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=86)** So we might have a method for our Person class called birthday where we increment the age.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=93)** Functions that create the object are called the constructor.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=98)** So you would instantiate an object using some code that you see here.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=103)** Alice gets new Person and then we pass a string to it.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=108)** This is all dependent on the definition of a class, which you'll do later.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=112)** But the important parts here are that after the assignment operator, we have the keyword new.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=119)** This tells PHP that we're about to create a new object based on the Person class.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=125)** And we can do this as many times as we want.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=128)** So we can create a second object called Bob.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=131)** We'll say Bob gets new Person and then pass the string Bob to it.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=136)** Similarly, we can get information from these objects based on the properties and methods available to us.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=145)** So if we have a method called get name, we could say echo Alice get name to print out Alice or echo Bob get name to print out Bob.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=159)** Notice that in these cases, when we're calling methods or properties that are based within classes, we need the arrow operator.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=169)** So we say Alice arrow get name or Bob arrow get name.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=174)** The same thing goes for sending information to an object.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=178)** We could say something like Alice arrow set age and then pass an integer or Bob arrow set age to set Bob's age.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=187)** And then we can reference those as well.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=189)** So that's the basic syntax of a class or object.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=193)** But it'll make more sense to see it in action.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/what-are-objects-and-classes?u=76281980&t=196)** So with the basic syntax down, let's write a simple Person class.

>

>

>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=89)** Casabona, Casabona, Casabona, Disney, Gates, Iger and Wozniack, but the first three people are not in alphabetical order, they all have the same last name, but Erin would go before Joe which would go before Rob.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=107)** So what else can we do here?
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=109)** Well, luckily, the spaceship operator can also sort arrays.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=115)** So what we'll do and this will be a fairly complex looking line.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=121)** So I'm just going to expand my editor here a little bit.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=126)** So we would say return and then I'm going to use the shorthand definition for arrays which is just simply square brackets.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=136)** So I would say square bracket a get last name comma a arrow get first name and then close that square bracket and I'll do the same thing over here.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=151)** Square bracket b arrow get last name comma b arrow get first name
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=163)** close square bracket.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=165)** So what this is saying is use the spaceship operator to first compare last names or the first element in the array and then compare the first name or the second element in the array.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=181)** Now if I save this, reduce the window size again and refresh, now the Casabonas are sorted properly and the rest of the array is, too.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-sort-an-array-of-objects?u=76281980&t=196)** So that's how you can use an anonymous function within the built-in usort function to sort an array of person objects.
