### 6. Including External Files

#### Organizing your code
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As you can imagine, writing lots of code in a single file can get unwieldly and slow to run and manage.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=7)** There is a better way. External files.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=11)** PHP offers a few ways for you to include files inside your code but they are all referred to as server side includes.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=20)** This, as the name suggests, includes all of the files in a single package that it sends to the browser.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=27)** That means there's only one HTTP request.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=31)** Unlike HTML, CSS, and JavaScript, which generally require one HTTP request per file, PHP does all of the processing, including the files on the server, and sends them as one.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=45)** There are several benefits to using server side includes in your applications.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=50)** First, it allows you to break up large sections of code.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=54)** You can put your classes in separate files, you can group functions together, and more.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=60)** As a result, you can keep output files separate from processing files.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=65)** So if you have heavy HTML files, then you might not want to put them in a class file or a file that requires a lot of functions.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=76)** You can separate them out.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=77)** You can also organize your website into different files, taking the reusable parts and templatizing them.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=85)** So far, we haven't talked much about the HTML output or organization side of PHP.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=93)** We were focused more on how PHP works.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=97)** But when you include files, PHP essentially inserts those files and the code wherever you include that file.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=105)** So let's say on line one we have some code, on line five we have more code, and on line three we include an external file.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=116)** PHP is essentially copying everything in that external file and putting it on line three.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=123)** That means that if you want to break your website into different parts like a header, a footer, and a sidebar, then you can.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=133)** You can use the same header and footer throughout the entire website.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=137)** And whenever you change them, they'll change across every page that includes them.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=142)** So if you have a website with 100 pages and you want to change one of the links in the navigation, you'd only have to do it once instead of 100 times.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=151)** It's up to you to figure out how to best organize your files.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=155)** And that might change based on the size and scope of the project you're working on.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/organizing-your-code?u=76281980&t=160)** But for now, let's take a closer look at how to use server side includes.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Env Vars:** php (6), html (3), http (2), css (1)
> **CLI Commands:** php (6)

> **Definitions:** is a  (1), means that (1)
> **Analogies:** imagine (1)

#### Using include and require
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are two ways to include external files in PHP.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=4)** They are with the built-in directives or functions, Include and Require.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=11)** There is a small distinction made because Include and Require don't have the parentheses around them, though you could write them that way.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=20)** They're not technically functions.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=22)** They're more like statements that you're making.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=26)** They both work the same way though, with the small difference that with Include, if the file doesn't exist, Include will throw a warning or non-terminating error, and Require we'll throw a compile error, which will stop the program from running.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=45)** So let's say that we have two files, Index and Variables, both are in the same directory.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=54)** What we can do is copy the variables from Index and paste them into the Variables dot PHP file.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=63)** We still need the opening PHP tag here, and then we'll save that.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=71)** And instead, we could say Require will require this because our variables are being used later.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=80)** So we don't want to cause an error, Require variables dot PHP.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=87)** Now, if we open this in the browser, we see our HTML is printed out with our variables.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=94)** And if we view the source, you'll see at the HTML, no traces of PHP here.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=101)** If you want to add a folder structure, you can do that too.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=105)** It would be the same as linking to HTML files.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=109)** So if we move the variables file into the INC or INK folder, first, we'll save this and go back to our browser.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=120)** Notice that we're getting a warning and then a fatal error because we required the Variables file.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=128)** So if we go back and fix this to add INC slash variables and then refresh everything is working properly.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=138)** You can also make, Include or Require statements within the classes and functions.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=143)** You should just remember that the code will be within the scope of wherever you include it.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=149)** So if we have a function here, we'll just say function INK.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=156)** And then we put the Require call inside the function and then go back to our page, we're getting an undefined variable because now the variables are within the scope of our function.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=172)** Delete those.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=174)** So you should remember that the code is being inserted wherever the Include file is added.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=179)** That way your PHP will definitely be valid after the insert.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=183)** One more note about Include and Require.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/using-include-and-require?u=76281980&t=186)** If you are using it to build website templates, you need to be mindful of the links as they appear to the user.
>

#### include_once and require_once
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are two other functions to help you include files, include once and require once.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=7)** These files work exactly the same as include and require, except that if the file in question has already been included, these functions return the value true and the file isn't included again.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=19)** This is great if you want to make sure you're not including a set of functions or a class more than once, because PHP throws an error if you try to redefine these functions or classes.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=32)** So let's take a look at a quick example.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=35)** You can see, I have my index page on the screen here and in the inc folder, there is a new file called functions.php.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=43)** In functions.php I've got two functions, pre-print and para print to do some of the things we've been doing a lot.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=53)** And if I go back to include, I can add it here on the page, I'll do it above the variables file inclusion.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=63)** And first I'll use require here.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=66)** So we'll say require inc/functions.php.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=73)** And just to see if that's working, we can delete this line and instead say, para print description.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=85)** So now we'll go to our browser.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=87)** We'll refresh the page and nothing's changed because the functions are working properly.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=94)** But let's say I forget what I'm doing for a minute and I realize, hey, I got to include my functions file, and I decide to do it in the variables file.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=104)** So I'll do require functions.php.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=109)** Notice I'm leaving off the inc because functions and variables are in the same directory.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=116)** Now, if I go back out to my website, I refresh and I have a fatal error, cannot redeclare para print, which was previously declared.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=127)** That's because I am requiring the functions file more than once.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=132)** So I'll change this to require once and I'll do the same thing here require once.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=139)** And just to show you what happens, we'll do a VAR dump here since this will return true if it's already been included.

>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=151)** I'll go back out and you can see we're no longer getting a fatal error.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/include-once-and-require-once?u=76281980&t=156)** And there is a true value, which means that at this point, online six of variables.php functions has already been included.

#### Namespacing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Illustrator] When you declare a variable, you can reassign values to it over and over again, even within the same scope.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=7)** But when it comes to functions and classes, PHP isn't so open to that, and for good reason.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=14)** Changing how a function works in the middle of a program would be like replacing your car engine with a bike pedals in the middle of the Daytona 500.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=23)** That also means you need to be mindful of the code already written, especially if you're working within a larger framework with lots of contributing developers, something like WordPress or Laravel.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=35)** You might not know all of the code that's written or could be written, and if your class or function names are not specific enough, you could unknowingly cause a site breaking errors.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=47)** A more recent development in writing PHP is Namespacing.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=52)** The idea here is that you might not have a class of functions, but it's common functionality that you don't want colliding with other code.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=62)** This will allow you to make your own scope for functions.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=67)** Let's look at a simple example.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=69)** Let's say that we have a function here called double, but it doesn't do what we'd expected to do.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=76)** It only increments the past value by two.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=80)** So we have function double A and then return A plus two.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=84)** Now, we can't later in the file redefined function to make it do what we need it to do.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=91)** If we tried to do that, which I'm doing right now, return A times two PHP will yell at us saying we can't redeclare double.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=104)** So what do we do?
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=106)** We can create a namespace.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=109)** Let's create a new file in our directory and we'll call it the Learning PHP Namespace.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=116)** So learningPHP.PHP.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=120)** The first thing that we want to do to create the namespace is actually define it.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=124)** So we'll add our PHP tag and then the next line will be the word namespace, and then whatever we want the namespace to be.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=134)** We'll call this learning PHP.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=137)** Now, we can define our new function and save this file.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=143)** Now, if we go back to index, let's include our namespace learningPHP.PHP add the semi-colon.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=155)** And now we can use our new double function.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=159)** The way we would do that is by adding the name of the namespace and then a backslash with the function, just like you would do in a file system or directory.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=171)** So here we can write echo P learningPHP/double five,
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=185)** and then add our closing paragraph tag.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=190)** And now when we refresh the page, we have our incorrect function and then our properly namespaced function.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/namespacing?u=76281980&t=200)** Namespaces can be very handy, especially if you're working on big projects or when you want to keep a set of functions or classes together.

> **CLI Commands:** php (8), make (2)

> **File Paths:** learningphp.php (2)

>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-build-a-simple-templating-system-for-the-provided-markup?u=76281980&t=137)** We don't necessarily need the PHP tags here in the opening because we're starting with HTML, but we do need to name this A.PHP file because we are referencing the variables.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-build-a-simple-templating-system-for-the-provided-markup?u=76281980&t=150)** Which brings us to the next step.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-build-a-simple-templating-system-for-the-provided-markup?u=76281980&t=153)** We want the variables to be included on every page so it makes sense to include them in the header.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-build-a-simple-templating-system-for-the-provided-markup?u=76281980&t=158)** So on the very first line here, I'm going to write require once.
>

### 7. Troubleshooting Your Code

#### Common debugging tools in PHP

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-debugging-tools-in-php?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Finding errors in code, especially when you're just starting out, isn't always the easiest thing to do.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-debugging-tools-in-php?u=76281980&t=6)** From syntax to runtime errors, you might spend some time troubleshooting, so it's good to look at what you'll come across.

> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-debugging-tools-in-php?u=76281980&t=93)** We haven't talked much about the PHP INI file so far, but it's basically a settings or configuration file for your server.

#### Types of PHP errors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are several different types of errors in PHP, and they will behave differently based on their severity.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=7)** Syntax or parse errors are generally typos or misused characters in your code.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=13)** The compiler will stop your code from running completely, and there will be no output with a parse error.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=20)** You can see we have one here on line four, where I forgot to escape a single quote.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=26)** The way this page is structured is there's a heading for each error, and then a paragraph after each error, to demonstrate whether or not output continues.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=37)** In this case, you can already see that my syntax highlighting is off and the line in question, line four has a red underline.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=49)** If I save this page and open it up in the browser, you can see before the parse error, we have a heading, but when I refresh, all of that goes away and there's a single parse error, that says unexpected 'VE' and then it tells you what it was expecting on line four.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=69)** That helps us figure out exactly where the bug is.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=72)** I know that there is a missing escape here.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=77)** And so once I do that, the underline goes away and the rest of my syntax highlighting is looking good.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=85)** Warnings are errors that will not terminate your code, but will tell you of a situation that might cause bigger problems down the line.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=94)** One example is a missing include file.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=96)** That will generate a warning that the file hasn't been included.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=100)** Which in and of itself, will not cause your program to terminate, but it could be problematic if you defined functions, which will cause another kind of error.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=109)** So you can see on line 10, I'm including a variables.php file that is not in my directory.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=117)** If we go back out to our page and refresh, you'll see that we have output them we have the warning, but the output continues.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=128)** The next kind of error is a fatal error.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=132)** And as the name implies, the fatal error will terminate or kill the program because the error causes a breaking issue.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=140)** The PHP manual calls, these errors, unrecoverable.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=144)** They will make your program work unexpectedly.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=147)** You can imagine the difference between a warning and a fatal error like this, a dent in your car is a warning.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=154)** The door shouldn't be dented, and it could cause an issue like your door not fully closing down the line, but right now your car still works.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=164)** But if you run out of gas, that's a fatal error because now your car will not drive.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=171)** Here's an example of a fatal error.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=173)** I'm calling a function called multiply that has not been defined.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=178)** So if we go to our page and refresh, and I scroll down to the fatal error section, you'll see that we have a fatal error called undefined function, multiply on line 14, and no other output happened after that.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=193)** The last official error in PHP is a notice.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=198)** This will tell you that something might be wrong, but it might not be either.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=203)** This is a keen to your check engine light or your gaslight, you're being told something doesn't seem right, but everything also looks like it's working normally.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=212)** An example of a notice is an undefined variable.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=216)** Perhaps you haven't defined it yet, and technically it's not breaking anything.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=221)** So let me comment out my fatal error here, so that we could see what a notice looks like.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=226)** And I'll add some output here, notice has code terminated.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=237)** I'll refresh, and then we get the notice that there's an undefined variable, but the code continues.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=244)** There are also a logic errors.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=247)** They aren't official PHP errors, but they occur because there are flow problems in your code.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=253)** All of your code can be devoid of the other errors and notices, but there's still a problem based on some assumption you might have made.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=261)** A great example of this as an infinite loop.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=263)** Infinite loops don't throw errors per se, but you've made an assumption about a terminating condition that never happens.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/types-of-php-errors?u=76281980&t=270)** So with these four types of errors defined, plus the logic errors, let's take a look at some specific examples.

#### Why is my page blank?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One common scenario that can be confusing if you've never seen it before is your website loading a completely blank page, no mark up or anything.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=10)** Now modern servers or even modern browsers might treat this error differently by showing a 500 error so you won't get a completely blank page but your page will be broken and you won't know why.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=24)** Outside of a completely blank page, you might have an error where you have some output but at some point it just stops.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=34)** And in any of these cases, this will happen if you have error reporting turned off in PHP.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=41)** This will be most common with syntax errors because they completely stop the code from compiling.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=46)** Remember that fatal errors terminate the code during runtime so any code that produced output before that fatal error will still show up.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=54)** Of course, if there's a fatal error before any output, the page will also be blank.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=59)** The best thing to do here is revisit the error reporting function you learned about earlier.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=64)** Or, if you have access to your PHP INI file, you want to make sure that error reporting is turned on to E all and display errors is also turned on.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=77)** If you modify your PHP INI file, you will need to restart your server for those changes to take effect, but also remember to turn these errors off for production.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=88)** Part of the reason to show a blank page instead of the error is your website is failing more gracefully so users don't see the errors but it's also for security.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/why-is-my-page-blank?u=76281980&t=99)** You don't want to accidentally expose information that can help malicious people attack your website or server.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** php (3), ini (2)
> **CLI Commands:** php (3), make (1)
> **Code Keywords:** function (1)
> **Best Practices:** remember to (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Common parse errors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When it comes to syntax errors, your code editor should do a lot of the work for you.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=5)** Still, if you don't have real time error checking or there's a lot of code to manage, you might miss certain syntax errors.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=13)** Here are some of the common ones you might come across.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=16)** On line two here, you have a missing semi-colon.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=20)** Pretty much every line needs to end in a semi-colon.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=24)** Starting out or if you have a lot of code, this one is easy to miss.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=29)** If we refresh, we'll see that there is an unexpected echo on line four, so you'll also notice that the error is not happening on line two, it's happening with the next line of code on line four.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=43)** That's something to keep in mind.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=45)** Usually when you see the word unexpected, you could be missing a semi-colon.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=50)** Incomplete quotes is a another error that you might come across and this could cause two different types of errors that might cause some confusion.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=61)** Here on line four, you'll see the statement echo hello, but we haven't defined echo as a constant.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=69)** In fact, we are intending to use it as a string, but we forgot the quotes.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=75)** So if we refresh our page, we'll see that we see a parse error, but it's on line eight.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=82)** Line eight does not have a parse error, the one above it does though, so just to make this error explicitly clear because this would be very hard to track down, let's get rid of the other errors.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=94)** We'll save this and then we'll refresh.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=97)** Now we see the actual error, which is a warning.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=100)** Undefined constant hello, assumed and then hello in quotes, this will throw an error in a future version of PHP.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=109)** So technically, if we had errors turned off, we would see nothing here, we would just see what we expected.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=115)** However, this is a warning because in future versions of PHP, undefined constants will not be treated as strings.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=123)** That's also why the other errors showed up first.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=128)** So now let's add those back in and we will put quotes around hello so that we can continue on this journey.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=138)** The other incomplete quote related error you might see results because you might have a misplaced or missing quote that confuses the compiler.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=148)** So in this case, we're missing the opening quote around the second value in our associated array.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=156)** If we refresh, we'll see unexpected comma T constant encapsed string.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=164)** This means that there's unexpected white space and this error could be confusing because it might not show up or it might say that it's on the last line of the file depending on the error.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=176)** So just keep that in mind as well.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=179)** The last couple of errors pertain to missing syntax for control structures.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=184)** So you can see that we have an error here.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=186)** There's a missing parenthesis.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=189)** If we refresh the page, it's another parse error that says unexpected curly brace here.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=196)** So we know that the error happens before that and similarly, if we get rid of the closing curly brace, we'll get an unexpected end of file and what that means is that the file randomly ended and the compilers not sure why.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=215)** So when you get either one of those, be sure to check your curly braces and your parentheses.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=222)** As you work through parse errors, you might notice that the error seems to often happen on the next line.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=230)** That's because the compiler doesn't know there's an error until it encounters unexpected code.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/common-parse-errors?u=76281980&t=236)** Be sure to read the error closely and look at the few lines proceeding the line mentioned in the error.

### 8. Processing Forms

#### GET vs. POST
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Processing forms is one of the most common things you'll probably do on your website in PHP.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=6)** But before we get into actually processing the forms, there are two methods for processing that you should know about, GET and POST.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=15)** This is associated with the method attribute in HTML, which determines how the form data should be sent.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=23)** GET is the default option, this method transmits data from one page to another as part of the URL.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=31)** So if I fill out this form, and then press Submit, you'll see that all of the data I just filled out shows up in the URL up here.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=42)** I've also created the page so that it prints out the resulting array.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=48)** To retrieve this information, you would need to use what's called a superglobal variable in PHP, and that's the GET array.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=57)** This superglobal has all of the information passed to our action page, which since it's not defined defaults to the same page the form is on, it's passed as an associative array, which you can see here.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=72)** The array has name, which is the name of the first form field and then the value, the same thing with email and reason.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=82)** You'll also notice that topics which are checkboxes and the movies, which is the multiselect box are passed as arrays as well.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=91)** So this is a multi dimensional array.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=94)** Finally, you'll notice that the submit button is also passed.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=98)** The other method is POST so if we change this method to POST and then let's add another condition up here, we'll say if not empty POST, var dump POST and then we will remove all of this,
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=122)** refresh and if we fill it out again and press Submit.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=129)** Now it's the POST array that's printing.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=133)** You'll notice that none of the information is passed in the URL here.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=138)** So how do you choose which to use and when?
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=141)** With GET, the data is entered by the user and visible in the URL.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=147)** So you should never pass sensitive data like passwords, you also shouldn't pass data that can be tampered with.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=156)** So if I do something like, question mark hello equals there that is now in the get array.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=166)** Using get and therefore allowing form data to be visible and mutable is great if you want to make the results shareable or if you want to let the user save them.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=179)** But on the other hand, the length of a GET request is limited the limit can range between 2000 and 8000 characters depending on the server and browser configuration.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=190)** There is no limit on results when using POST and it's more secure, but the results cannot be shared nor can the result of a specific form submission be saved without extra processing.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/get-vs-post?u=76281980&t=204)** So with that, let's get into processing some forms.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** post (7), url (4), php (2), html (1)
> **API Endpoints:** get  (4), post
 (3), post  (2)
> **Code Keywords:** let (3), pass (2), finally, (1), var (1), this, (1)
> **CLI Commands:** php (2), make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Best Practices:** you should never (1)

#### Form validation: Making sure required fields are filled in
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/form-validation-making-sure-required-fields-are-filled-in?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/form-validation-making-sure-required-fields-are-filled-in?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Form validation is mostly handled with HTML and JavaScript these days.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/form-validation-making-sure-required-fields-are-filled-in?u=76281980&t=5)** But it's still important to make sure you're getting proper data on the backend because people could have JavaScript turned off or they could be using an older browser or the browser validation might not validate the same way you want to validate.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/form-validation-making-sure-required-fields-are-filled-in?u=76281980&t=22)** So the first way to validate on the backend is to make sure all required fields are filled out and we're going to do this in line so that we can give the user the proper message right next to the form field instead of making them go to a different page, telling them the form wasn't right and then having them have to go back to the page.

#### Form validation: Appropriate input
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/form-validation-appropriate-input?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/form-validation-appropriate-input?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Another type of validation you'll want to do on the server is making sure that data submitted is appropriate data.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/form-validation-appropriate-input?u=76281980&t=6)** For example, you'll want to make sure the email address is an actual email address.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/form-validation-appropriate-input?u=76281980&t=12)** Luckily, browsers do a lot of this for us now if we use the right input type.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/form-validation-appropriate-input?u=76281980&t=18)** For example, the email input type will validate an email address before the form is submitted.

>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/form-validation-appropriate-input?u=76281980&t=28)** The way you'd accomplish this is by comparing the input to a description of what the input should look like.

> **[7:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/sending-the-form-data-in-an-email?u=76281980&t=422)** So it's worth reading up on the PHP manual page to see if you're having any trouble.

#### Solution: Process a simple form and send the email
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=7)** Now we're not going to type this one out line by line because there's a lot to cover and you can get this entire working file in the exercise files, but there are a few things worth pointing out.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=18)** First, I assign all of the data to a new array called form data just to keep it safe and sound in case something happens to post.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=27)** Not totally necessary, but a practice that I started doing a long time ago.

>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=37)** Now we could set it to null but that's going to cause a lot of issues.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=41)** So what we're doing first is we're seeing if the form has been filled out at all.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=46)** If empty post, then the form is not complete and we can set it to false.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=52)** However, if the form has been filled out, we'll set this to null with the understanding that during our required checks, the form is complete, variable could be set to false.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=64)** If it's never set to false, then it must be true.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=68)** Then we have two functions.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=70)** One is check required and it gets a value and the other is check email and it gets an email address.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=77)** Both of these are validation functions.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=80)** Check required, make sure that after we trim our string, there's still a value in it.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=86)** If there's not, we grab the global variable form is complete and set it to false.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=94)** Similarly, with check email, we have our rejects.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=97)** We make sure that the email address is in a proper format and if it's not, we grab the global form is complete variable and set it to false.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=107)** If after these functions run, form is complete is never set to false, then we have a fully filled out and valid form.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=116)** We're going to skip over send message for a minute and just check out the form that we have.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=122)** Our error messages are in line.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=126)** Our check required and check email are also in line.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=131)** We're using the proper input types and the required attributes and again, we're checking the message, which is also required.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=142)** Now at the bottom here, we're checking to see if form is complete is null.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=147)** If it's not null, then use the current value, which should be false.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=152)** If it is null, then we have a fully valid form and we'll set it to true and once we do that, we can send the message.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=161)** Now I'm not using the null coalescing operator here or the Elvis operator because I am on a slightly older version of php on my local environment and those two operators are four later versions of php, so I want to make sure that this was fully working no matter what version of php you happen to be using.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=183)** Now let's go back to the send mail function.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=186)** So it accepts an array of data and then it sanitizes everything.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=192)** So for name, we trimmed the name, we stripped the tags and we replaced the quotes with escaped quotes.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=202)** With the email address, we know it's technically a valid email address already, but we filter to make sure it's actually proper.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=212)** We set our two fields.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=214)** We set our from field.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=216)** We don't need to sanitize reason because it is a select box.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=222)** Technically we probably should because if somebody tampers with the html before the information is sent, they could insert malicious scripts, but we don't here.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=232)** We set our subject.

> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/solution-process-a-simple-form-and-send-the-email?u=76281980&t=253)** So this is a fully working form with lots of validation built in.

### 9. Advanced Topics

#### Reading files in PHP
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=0)** - One of the ways to store and access data though it should absolutely be non-secure data, is by reading and writing to files.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=9)** PHP allows you to read the contents of a file and if you have access to it, write to it as well.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=15)** So first let's look at a couple of examples of reading files.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=19)** On the screen here you see I have a file called treasure-island.txt, with a selection of lines from the Robert Louis Stevenson novel.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=31)** There are two ways that we can read this file.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=34)** The first is using a built-in function called readfile.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=38)** So I can say readfile and then give it a file name, treasure-island.txt.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=49)** This function simply grabs the contents of a file and puts it into the output buffer which is in most cases, immediately printed out onto the screen.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=61)** Let's refresh our page here and see the results.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=64)** You can see it's the entire file.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=67)** And if we View Source, there is no formatting to it but it did break it up by lines.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=75)** We just don't see that in the browser.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=80)** But in case you don't immediately want to print out the results of a file on screen, there's actually another function that can help us.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=89)** And that is file_get_contents, which reads the entire file as a single string, stripping out any formatting and line breaks.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=102)** So again if we save this, there's nothing on the screen here, we'd actually have to echo it.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=109)** So we can store the file contents into a variable using get file contents.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=116)** Perhaps the best way to get the contents of a file is with the file function.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=122)** Which will read the entire file as an array where each line is a new entry into the array.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=129)** So we can say lines, get file, and then the name of the file.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=135)** If we do a simple var_dump here, we could see that each line gets its own entry in the array.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=145)** So you can loop through this with a for each loop if you would like.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=150)** But this is for more than just output.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=153)** And it could be more than just files accessible on your server.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=157)** This could be for any text file including web pages.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=160)** So let's look at another example.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=163)** I'll comment out var_dump here so that we can better read what we're about to do.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=170)** So let's make a variable called casabona and we'll use file_get_contents.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=178)** But this time, we'll call a web page.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=180)** We'll call my homepage.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=185)** Then if we var_dump casabona, you can see we've retrieved the entirety of the HTML from my homepage.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=200)** Now, this could be useful if I want to see if a site for example is using WordPress, I could say if and then use a string search function called strpos for string position.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=214)** And then I could search the text casabona for the text wp-content, which is an indicator that WordPress is being run and then I can echo, this website is using WordPress.

>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=237)** This website is using WordPress.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=239)** There can be countless applications for this.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/reading-files-in-php?u=76281980&t=242)** But just remember not to violate any copyrights by stealing or scraping content you don't have permission to do so on.

#### Maintaining state with cookies
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Cookies are a great way for PHP to remember the state of a user's browser.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=5)** This can be anything for any information like their name or if they're logged in.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=12)** See by default, PHP doesn't maintain state.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=17)** so if you move from page to page, unless you do something to explicitly remember what a user did on the last page, PHP will not do that automatically for you and cookies persist as long as the browser stays open.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=31)** If you've ever had a website remember some setting, like being logged in, that website was likely using a cookie.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=39)** To set a cookie in PHP, you would use the function set cookie.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=44)** This accepts a name, a value and an optional expiration.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=49)** So let's create a cookie that tells us if the user has visited the site before.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=56)** We'll set this to true and we won't set an expiration date.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=60)** So we'll just let it stay as long as the browser stays open.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=64)** To check that cookie, we would use the cookie super global array.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=69)** So let's say you want to print a message to the user if they have already visited the site.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=77)** We could say if is set $_cookie visited
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=87)** then and we'll use the alternative syntax here.

> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=90)** We could say welcome back.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=95)** If they are new visitors, then we can use the alternative syntax again and say welcome.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=107)** Okay, so now if we refresh this page, we see welcome back because that cookie was set.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=117)** If I open this page in a private browser, we see welcome and then if we refresh, we see welcome back.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=127)** So everything here is working as expected.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=131)** If later you want to delete the cookie, you would use the same set cookie function with an expiration date for the past.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=140)** So let's on the bottom of this page, destroy the cookie we'll say, set cookie visited.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=152)** We'll give it any value 'cause we're going to expire it.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=155)** Time - 3600 so this is just the current time minus some number of milliseconds.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=163)** So if we refresh, you'll see we get a warning here, cannot modify header information.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=168)** This is important, we can't set cookies after information has been printed on the screen.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=174)** So if we want to set a cookie we need to do it before anything is printed on the screen.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=179)** So let's say if it's set cookie visited, maybe we only want to show the welcome back message once.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=185)** We can immediately destroy the cookie.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=189)** So now if we refresh, we'll see just the welcome message.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=194)** Sending cookies is great if you want to remember some sort of information but you definitely need to do it before you print any information out on the screen.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=202)** The drawback of cookies is that they are accessible on a user's computer.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=208)** So they can temper with the cookies if they want If I go in here and I look at the visited cookie, I can block or remove it .
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-cookies?u=76281980&t=219)** Another way to maintain state that can't be tampered by the user is sessions.

#### Maintaining state with sessions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Cookies are great for maintaining state, but one problem with this is they're on the user's computer.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=6)** That means the user might be able to modify the value of a cookie.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=10)** That's fine if you're storing simple data like their name or if they're logged in, but what if you're storing something like shopping cart data or other information that shouldn't be modified by the user?
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=22)** That's where sessions come in.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=24)** Sessions like cookies expire when the browser closes, but the sessions are stored on the server, which users can access.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=35)** Another benefit of sessions is they can be set at any time, unlike cookies, which need to be set before anything is output onto the screen.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=46)** So for our sessions example, let's modify our mailing list.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=53)** Let's say we only want to show this form to people who haven't signed up for the mailing list.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=59)** That way, if people who have signed up visit the page, they won't be able to sign up again.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=65)** So the first thing we'll do in our mailing.php file, which you can get in the exercise files, is at the very top of the page, we're going to write session start.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=78)** This is going to tell php that we're starting a new session for this browser and to store information where you can.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=87)** Now in our join mailing list function, we're going to add at the bottom, after everything is printed out, a new session variable.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=102)** So we're going to use the session super global array for this, very similar to the cookies super global array.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=109)** So we'll say dollar sign underscore session.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=113)** We're just going to set a new key here called signed up and we're going to set it to true.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=122)** So now we've set a new sessions variable after the user signs up.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=128)** Now we can do this.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=131)** We can say at the top of the join mailing list header if is set session signed up and so we're going to make sure this is set and it's actually true and actually we want it to not be true.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=154)** So and if it's set and it's not true, we're going to show the form.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=162)** Again, we're going to use alternative syntax for this because we are writing a large block of HTML.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=171)** Okay, so this says if is set session signed up and not session signed up, which means it's not true, then show the form.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=180)** So we'll save this.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=181)** We'll go back to our mailing list form.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=185)** Our mailing list form has disappeared and that's because we're checking to see if the session variable has been set, which it hasn't.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=193)** So we can actually say if not is set or not session signed up, so this is going to say, if the session variable signed up has not been set or it doesn't evaluate to true, print the form.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=211)** So let's go ahead and fill this out.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=216)** We'll click submit and now it says thanks we're on the list and the form is still showing up, but that is because we set the session after we print this message, so if we refresh the page, the form is gone.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=234)** So that's how we use sessions.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=238)** If at any point you want to destroy a session, you can use the session destroy directive, like this.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=249)** This will kill the session and any data associated with it.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=255)** So with that, you have a basic understanding of how sessions work.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-php-4/maintaining-state-with-sessions?u=76281980&t=259)** Sessions and cookies are both very useful for temporarily saving data, but if you want something more long-term, you'll need to use a database or something similar.
